# Debug Q&A: Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/cilium--a2f97aa8 (mirror of cilium/cilium)
**Task Type:** Debug Q&A (investigation only — no code changes)

## Background

Cilium is a Kubernetes CNI (Container Network Interface) plugin that uses eBPF (Extended Berkeley Packet Filter) programs to enforce network policies and route traffic. In a multi-node Kubernetes cluster, each node runs its own Cilium agent which compiles, loads, and attaches eBPF programs to enforce policies.

## Behavior Observation

**What happens:** When an eBPF program fails to compile or load on one Kubernetes node (e.g., due to a kernel verifier rejection, compilation error, or incompatible kernel features), the other nodes in the cluster continue to enforce their network policies normally. The failing node may log errors or degrade to a fallback mode, but the failure doesn't propagate cluster-wide.

**Why is this notable?** Many distributed systems that rely on shared state or centralized configuration can fail cluster-wide when one node encounters a problem. Cilium's architecture ensures that eBPF datapath failures are isolated to the affected node.

## Questions

Answer ALL of the following questions to explain this behavior:

### Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle

How are eBPF programs compiled, loaded, and attached on each node independently?
- What component is responsible for compiling eBPF programs on each node?
- How does node-specific configuration (kernel version, enabled features, etc.) affect compilation?
- At what point in the lifecycle does the eBPF program become node-local vs. cluster-wide?

### Q2: Deployment Architecture

How does Cilium's deployment model ensure per-node isolation?
- How is the Cilium agent deployed across the cluster (DaemonSet, static pods, etc.)?
- What happens when one node's Cilium agent fails to initialize its eBPF programs?
- How does the control plane vs. data plane split contribute to isolation?

### Q3: Policy Distribution vs. Enforcement

Cilium network policies are cluster-wide resources (CRDs), but enforcement is per-node. How does this work?
- How are CiliumNetworkPolicy resources distributed to each node?
- What component on each node translates policies into eBPF bytecode?
- Why doesn't a compilation failure on one node block policy distribution to other nodes?

### Q4: eBPF Map Scoping and State Isolation

eBPF programs use maps (hash tables, arrays) to store state. How is map state isolated across nodes?
- Are eBPF maps node-local or cluster-wide?
- What mechanisms (BPF filesystem pinning, namespaces) ensure map isolation?
- If a node fails to create or update a map, how does that affect other nodes' packet processing?

## Output Requirements

Write your answer to `/logs/agent/investigation.md` with the following structure:

```
# Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation

## Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle
<answer with specific file paths, class names, and function references>

## Q2: Deployment Architecture
<answer with specific file paths, class names, and function references>

## Q3: Policy Distribution vs. Enforcement
<answer with specific file paths, class names, and function references>

## Q4: eBPF Map Scoping and State Isolation
<answer with specific file paths, class names, and function references>

## Evidence
<consolidated list of supporting file paths and line references>
```

72-assistanttext-
The investigation report has been written to `/logs/agent/investigation.md`. Here's a summary of the findings:

---

## Summary of Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation

### Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle
Each node's Cilium agent compiles eBPF programs **locally** using `clang`, invoked by `compile()` in `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go`. The compiler probes the **local kernel** via `getBPFCPU()` to select the correct BPF ISA (`v1`/`v2`/`v3`), embeds node-specific headers (device IPs, feature flags) written by `Reinitialize()`, and caches compiled ELF objects in `objectCache`. Programs are loaded into the **local kernel** via `reloadEndpoint()` → `bpf.LoadAndAssign()`. No cross-node coordination occurs at any step.

### Q2: Deployment Architecture
Cilium runs as a **DaemonSet** (`install/kubernetes/cilium/templates/cilium-agent/daemonset.yaml`), guaranteeing one independent agent pod per node. When one node's agent fails to compile/load eBPF (error returned from `Reinitialize()` or `realizeBPFState()`), the error is local — only that node's agent is affected. The control plane (Kubernetes API + etcd) distributes policy CRDs, but the data plane (BPF programs and maps) is entirely per-node.

### Q3: Policy Distribution vs. Enforcement
Each node's `policyWatcher` (`pkg/policy/k8s/watcher.go`) independently watches the Kubernetes API for CNP events. On receiving an event, `onUpsert()` adds the policy to the **local** `Repository`, triggering endpoint regeneration on that node only. `policyCache.updateSelectorPolicy()` resolves policy locally; compilation and kernel loading happen entirely within the node. A compilation failure on node A does not touch node B's watch stream, policy repository, or kernel.

### Q4: eBPF Map Scoping and State Isolation
All BPF maps are pinned to the node-loc

//\n248: // This code assumes that the agent was upgraded from a prior version while maintaining\n249: // the same list of managed physical devices. This ensures that all tc bpf filters get\n250: // replaced using the naming convention of the 'current' agent build. For example,\n251: // before 1.13, most filters were named e.g. bpf_host.o:[to-host], to be changed to\n252: // cilium-<device> in 1.13, then to cil_to_host-<device> in 1.14. err != nil {\n272: \t\t\tlog.WithError(err).WithField(logfields.Device, l.Attrs().Name)\n273: \t\t}\n274: \n275: \t\tingressFilters, err := safenetlink.FilterList(l, directionToParent(dirIngress))\n276: \t\tif err != nil {\n277: \t\t\treturn fmt.Errorf(\"listing ingress filters: %w\", err)\n278: \t\t}\n279: \t\tfor _, filter := range ingressFilters {\n280: \t\t\tif bpfFilter, ok := filter.(*netlink.BpfFilter); ok {\n281: \t\t\t\tif strings.HasPrefix(bpfFilter.Name, symbolFromHostNetdevEp) {\n282: \t\t\t\t\tingressDevs = append(ingressDevs, l)\n283: \t\t\t\t}\n284: \t\t\t}\n285: \t\t}\n286: \n287: \t\tegressFilters, err := safenetlink.FilterList(l, directionToParent(dirEgress))\n288: \t\tif err != nil {\n289: \t\t\treturn fmt.Errorf(\"listing egress filters: %w\", err)\n290: \t\t}\n291: \t\tfor _, filter := range egressFilters {\n292: \t\t\tif bpfFilter, ok := filter.(*netlink.BpfFilter);   "content": "# Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation\n\n## Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle\n\n### Component Responsible for Compilation\n\nThe **`loader`** struct in `pkg/datapath/loader/loader.go` (line 74) is the central component that orchestrates eBPF compilation, loading, and attachment on each node. It wraps the `objectCache` (defined in `pkg/datapath/loader/cache.go`) which amortizes compilation across endpoints with the same configuration.\n\nThe compilation itself is performed by **`clang`** (the LLVM C compiler), invoked via the `compile()` function in `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go` (line 170). The compiler is a node-local process \u2013 it runs as a subprocess of the Cilium agent on each node and produces BPF ELF object files.\n\n### Node-Specific Configuration Affecting Compilation\n\nNode-specific factors are embedded into BPF compilation in several ways:\n\n1. **CPU/ISA capabilities**: `getBPFCPU()` (`compile.go:135`) probes the local kernel at startup (via `probes.HaveV3ISA()`, `probes.HaveV2ISA()`, `probes.HaveProgramHelper()`) to select the BPF instruction set (`v1`, `v2`, or `v3`). This is passed as `-mcpu=<version>` to clang.\n\n2. **Number of CPUs**: `compile()` calls `ebpf.PossibleCPU()` and embeds the result as `-D__NR_CPUS__=<n>` (compile.go:191).\n\n3. **Node configuration header**: `Reinitialize()` (`base.go:382`) writes a `node_config.h` header via `l.writeNodeConfigHeader(cfg)` (base.go:465), embedding the node's IP addresses, device indices, and feature flags. This header is included by all BPF programs at compile time.\n\n4. **Template hashing**: `objectCache.fetchOrCompile()` (`cache.go:167`) hashes the combination of `LocalNodeConfiguration` and `EndpointConfiguration` to produce a cache key. Endpoints with different configurations get different compiled objects.\n\n### When eBPF Programs Become Node-Local\n\neBPF programs are entirely node-local from the moment compilation begins. The full lifecycle:\n\n1. `Endpoint.regenerateBPF()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:379`) calls `realizeBPFState()`.\n2. `realizeBPFState()` (`bpf.go:548`) calls `e.owner.Orchestrator().ReloadDatapath()`.\n3. `loader.ReloadDatapath()` (`loader.go:700`) calls `l.templateCache.fetchOrCompile()` to compile (or reuse a cached) ELF object, then calls `reloadEndpoint()` or `reloadHostEndpoint()`.\n4. `reloadEndpoint()` (`loader.go:511`) calls `bpf.LoadAndAssign()` to load the program into the **local kernel's** BPF subsystem and attach it to the endpoint's veth interface via `attachSKBProgram()`.\n\nThere is no coordination with other nodes at any point in this path. The program enters the node-local kernel and never communicates with other nodes' kernels.\n\n---\n\n## Q2: Deployment Architecture\n\n### DaemonSet Deployment\n\nCilium is deployed as a **Kubernetes DaemonSet** (`install/kubernetes/cilium/templates/cilium-agent/daemonset.yaml`, kind: DaemonSet). This guarantees exactly one `cilium-agent` pod per node. Each agent pod runs with host network access and elevated privileges to manipulate the node's kernel BPF subsystem.\n\nThe Kubernetes documentation for this is captured in `Documentation/network/kubernetes/concepts.rst` (lines 16\u201323): *\"A DaemonSet resource: describes the Cilium pod that is deployed to each Kubernetes node. This pod runs the cilium-agent and associated daemons.\"*\n\n### What Happens When One Node Fails to Initialize eBPF\n\nWhen `Reinitialize()` (`base.go:382`) fails (e.g., compilation error, kernel verifier rejection), the error is returned to the caller of `Reinitialize()`. The failure is **local** to that node's agent process:\n\n- The failing node logs the error and may enter a degraded state (endpoints remain without BPF programs or use previously loaded programs).\n- The Kubernetes control plane observes the pod's liveness/readiness probes and may restart the agent.\n- Other nodes' agents are independent processes running in their own DaemonSet pods; they never see the failure.\n\nPer-endpoint failures in `realizeBPFState()` (`bpf.go:548`) also return errors locally; at `bpf.go:566-572`, the error is logged with `e.getLogger().WithError(err).Error(\"Error while reloading endpoint BPF program\")` and returned, triggering endpoint-level error handling without affecting other endpoints or nodes.\n\n### Control Plane vs. Data Plane Split\n\n- **Control plane** (Kubernetes API server, etcd, Cilium operator): distributes policy objects as CRDs cluster-wide. This path does not involve eBPF.\n- **Data plane** (per-node cilium-agent): translates policies into BPF programs and maps. This is entirely local to each node.\n\nThe data plane is isolated because BPF programs are loaded into the node's own Linux kernel via `bpf.LoadAndAssign()` (which calls `ebpf.NewCollection()` internally), writing to the local `/sys/fs/bpf` filesystem. No cross-node kernel calls are made.\n\n---\n\n## Q3: Policy Distribution vs. Enforcement\n\n### How CiliumNetworkPolicy Resources Are Distributed\n\nCNP resources are Kubernetes CRDs stored in etcd and distributed via the Kubernetes API server. Each cilium-agent runs a local **`policyWatcher`** goroutine (`pkg/policy/k8s/watcher.go:71`, `watchResources()`) that subscribes to Kubernetes resource events for:\n- `CiliumNetworkPolicy` (CNP)\n- `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy` (CCNP)\n- `NetworkPolicy` (Kubernetes native)\n- `CiliumCIDRGroup`\n\nEvents are delivered independently to **each** agent via Kubernetes watch streams. Every node's agent watches the same API server but processes events independently and concurrently.\n\n### Per-Node Translation to eBPF Bytecode\n\nThe `policyWatcher.onUpsert()` (`cilium_network_policy.go:23`) receives a CNP event and calls `resolveCiliumNetworkPolicyRefs()`, which ultimately calls the `PolicyImporter` to add the policy to the local `Repository` (`pkg/policy/repository.go`).\n\nWhen policy changes, affected endpoints are queued for regeneration. `Endpoint.regenerateBPF()` \u2192 `runPreCompilationSteps()` (`bpf.go:593`) calls `e.regeneratePolicy()` \u2192 `policyCache.updateSelectorPolicy()` (`distillery.go:73`) \u2192 `cache.repo.resolvePolicyLocked()` to compute endpoint-specific policy. The resulting `selectorPolicy` is then compiled (via `objectCache.fetchOrCompile`) into BPF bytecode and loaded into the local kernel.\n\n### Why a Compilation Failure on One Node Doesn't Block Others\n\nEach node's agent independently:\n1. Watches the Kubernetes API for policy events.\n2. Translates those events into a local `Repository` and `selectorPolicy`.\n3. Compiles and loads BPF programs into its own kernel.\n\nSteps 2 and 3 are entirely local. If clang fails on node A (e.g., kernel version too old for a required feature), node A's endpoint logs the error and the endpoint remains in an error state. Node B's agent independently performs steps 1\u20133 using **its own** kernel capabilities and local `getBPFCPU()` probing. The Kubernetes API watch stream is read-only and stateless from each agent's perspective \u2014 a failure on node A's agent does not affect the watch stream that node B reads.\n\nThe `policyWatcher` (`watcher.go`) on each node is purely a consumer of Kubernetes events; there is no producer/consumer relationship between nodes.\n\n---\n\n## Q4: eBPF Map Scoping and State Isolation\n\n### Maps Are Node-Local\n\nAll BPF maps used by Cilium are **node-local** \u2014 they live in the node's kernel memory and are accessible only to processes on that node. They are not shared across the network.\n\n### BPF Filesystem Pinning and Namespaces\n\nMaps are pinned to the **BPF filesystem (bpffs)**, mounted at `/sys/fs/bpf` (constant `BPFFSRoot = \"/sys/fs/bpf\"` in `pkg/defaults/defaults.go:96`). The global map directory is `/sys/fs/bpf/tc/globals` (`TCGlobalsPath = \"tc/globals\"`, defaults.go:103).\n\nPinning is handled in `bpf.LoadAndAssign()` with `ebpf.MapOptions{PinPath: bpf.TCGlobalsPath()}` (loader.go:347, 394, 447, 516). Once pinned, maps survive agent restarts but remain local to the node.\n\nPer-endpoint maps are named using `LocalMapName()` / `LocalMapPath()` (`bpffs_linux.go:107-114`):\n```go\nfunc LocalMapName(name string, id uint16) string {\n    return fmt.Sprintf(\"%s%05d\", name, id)\n}\n```\nFor example, the per-endpoint policy map is `cilium_policy_v2_<endpoint_id>` (MapName = `\"cilium_policy_v2_\"` in `policymap.go:34`). The path is `bpf.LocalMapPath(policymap.MapName, e.ID)` (`endpoint/bpf.go:88`).\n\nThe `lxcmap` (global endpoint table, `MapName = \"cilium_lxc\"`, `lxcmap.go:21`) is also node-local \u2014 it maps endpoint IPs to their BPF metadata on that specific node.\n\n### Isolation Mechanism\n\nThe bpffs is a per-node host filesystem. The Cilium agent pod mounts the host's `/sys/fs/bpf` via `hostPath` volumes in the DaemonSet spec. Each node's bpffs is physically separate.\n\nWhen `reloadEndpoint()` loads programs, it pins maps into `/sys/fs/bpf/tc/globals/<mapname>` on the local node's filesystem. No other node can see or modify these pins.\n\nThe per-endpoint bpffs directory structure is managed by `bpffsEndpointLinksDir()` and `bpffsEndpointDir()` (referenced in loader.go:546, 766, 770). When an endpoint is deleted, `Unload()` (`loader.go:735`) removes these directories with `bpf.Remove()`.\n\n### Effect of Map Failure on Other Nodes\n\nIf a node fails to create or update a BPF map (e.g., out of memory, kernel resource limits), the error surfaces only in that node's agent:\n- `policymap.OpenOrCreate(e.policyMapPath())` (`bpf.go:739`) returns an error if map creation fails; `runPreCompilationSteps()` returns the error, aborting regeneration for that endpoint on that node only.\n- `lxcmap.WriteEndpoint()` (`bpf.go:473`) failure similarly returns a local error.\n- Other nodes' agents continue to use their own independently-created maps; they are completely unaffected.\n\nThe kernel BPF map subsystem is per-node. There is no cluster-wide BPF map synchronization mechanism \u2014 Cilium's shared state (identity allocation, node IPs) is maintained in etcd/KVStore and propagated to each node's **user-space** agent, which then programs its own kernel maps locally.\n\n---\n\n## Evidence\n\n| File | Lines | Relevance |\n|------|-------|-----------|\n| `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go` | 134\u2013157, 170\u2013197 | `getBPFCPU()` probes local kernel; `compile()` invokes clang locally |\n| `pkg/datapath/loader/cache.go` | 113\u2013222 | `objectCache.build()` and `fetchOrCompile()` \u2014 per-node compilation with local caching |\n| `pkg/datapath/loader/loader.go` | 74\u201389, 700\u2013732 | `loader` struct; `ReloadDatapath()` attaches programs to local interfaces |\n| The program enters the node-local kernel and never communicates with other nodes' kernels.\n\n---\n\n## Q2: Deployment Architecture\n\n### DaemonSet Deployment\n\nCilium is deployed as a **Kubernetes DaemonSet** (`install/kubernetes/cilium/templates/cilium-agent/daemonset.yaml`, kind: DaemonSet at line 16). This guarantees exactly one `cilium-agent` pod per node. Each agent pod runs with host network access and elevated privileges to manipulate the node's kernel BPF subsystem.\n\nThis is also documented in `Documentation/network/kubernetes/concepts.rst` (lines 16\u201323): *\"A DaemonSet resource: describes the Cilium pod that is deployed to each Kubernetes node. This pod runs the cilium-agent and associated daemons.\"*\n\n### What Happens When One Node Fails to Initialize eBPF\n\nWhen `Reinitialize()` (`base.go:382`) fails (e.g., compilation error, kernel verifier rejection), the error is returned to the caller. The failure is **local** to that node's agent process:\n\n- The failing node logs the error and may enter a degraded state (endpoints remain without BPF programs, or previously loaded programs continue running).\n- The Kubernetes control plane observes liveness/readiness probe failures and may restart only that agent pod.\n- Other nodes' agents are independent processes running in their own DaemonSet pods; they never see the failure.\n\nPer-endpoint failures in `realizeBPFState()` (`bpf.go:548`) return errors locally. At `bpf.go:566-572`, the error is logged with `e.getLogger().WithError(err).Error(\"Error while reloading endpoint BPF program\")` and returned to the endpoint's regeneration context, without affecting other endpoints or any remote node.\n\n### Control Plane vs. Data Plane Split\n\n- **Control plane** (Kubernetes API server, etcd, Cilium operator): distributes policy objects as CRDs cluster-wide. This path involves no eBPF.\n- **Data plane** (per-node cilium-agent): translates policies into BPF programs and maps. This is entirely local to each node.\n\nThe data plane is isolated because BPF programs are loaded into the node's own Linux kernel via `bpf.LoadAndAssign()`, writing pin files to the local `/sys/fs/bpf` filesystem. No cross-node kernel calls are made at any point.\n\n---\n\n## Q3: Policy Distribution vs. Enforcement\n\n### How CiliumNetworkPolicy Resources Are Distributed\n\nCNP resources are Kubernetes CRDs stored in etcd and distributed via the Kubernetes API server. Each cilium-agent runs a local **`policyWatcher`** goroutine (`pkg/policy/k8s/watcher.go:71`, `watchResources()`) that independently subscribes to Kubernetes resource events for:\n- `CiliumNetworkPolicy` (CNP)\n- `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy` (CCNP)\n- `NetworkPolicy` (Kubernetes native)\n- `CiliumCIDRGroup`\n\nEvents are delivered independently to **each** agent via Kubernetes watch streams. Every node's agent watches the same API server but processes events independently in its own goroutine.\n\n### Per-Node Translation to eBPF Bytecode\n\nThe `policyWatcher.onUpsert()` (`cilium_network_policy.go:23`) receives a CNP event and calls `resolveCiliumNetworkPolicyRefs()`, which calls the local `PolicyImporter` to add the policy to the node-local `Repository` (`pkg/policy/repository.go`).\n\nWhen policy changes, affected endpoints are queued for regeneration. The path is:\n- `Endpoint.regenerateBPF()` -> `runPreCompilationSteps()` (`bpf.go:593`)\n- calls `e.regeneratePolicy()` -> `policyCache.updateSelectorPolicy()` (`distillery.go:73`)\n- calls `cache.repo.resolvePolicyLocked()` to compute endpoint-specific policy locally\n- the resulting `selectorPolicy` is compiled (via `objectCache.fetchOrCompile`) into BPF bytecode and loaded into the **local** kernel\n\n### Why a Compilation Failure on One Node Doesn't Block Others\n\nEach node's agent independently:\n1. Watches the Kubernetes API for policy events.\n2. Translates those events into a local `Repository` and `selectorPolicy`.\n3. Compiles and loads BPF programs into its own kernel.\n\nSteps 2 and 3 are entirely local. If clang fails on node A (e.g., kernel too old for a required BPF feature), node A's endpoint logs the error and remains in an error state. Node B's agent independently performs steps 1\u20133 using **its own** kernel capabilities and local `getBPFCPU()` probe results.\n\nThe Kubernetes API watch stream is a read-only, stateless stream from each agent's perspective \u2014 a failure in node A's agent process does not affect the stream that node B reads, nor node B's local policy repository or BPF kernel state.\n\n---\n\n## Q4: eBPF Map Scoping and State Isolation\n\n### Maps Are Node-Local\n\nAll BPF maps used by Cilium are **node-local** \u2014 they live in the node's kernel memory and are accessible only to processes on that node. They are not shared across the network or between nodes in any way.\n\n### BPF Filesystem Pinning\n\nMaps are pinned to the **BPF filesystem (bpffs)**, mounted at `/sys/fs/bpf` (constant `BPFFSRoot = \"/sys/fs/bpf\"` in `pkg/defaults/defaults.go:96`). The global map directory is `/sys/fs/bpf/tc/globals` (`TCGlobalsPath = \"tc/globals\"`, defaults.go:103).\n\nPinning is performed in `bpf.LoadAndAssign()` with `ebpf.MapOptions{PinPath: bpf.TCGlobalsPath()}` (loader.go:347, 394, 447, 516). Once pinned, maps survive agent restarts but remain strictly local to the node's bpffs.\n\nPer-endpoint maps use `LocalMapName()` / `LocalMapPath()` (`bpffs_linux.go:107-114`):\n```go\nfunc LocalMapName(name string, id uint16) string {\n    return fmt.Sprintf(\"%s%05d\", name, id)\n}\nfunc LocalMapPath(name string, id uint16) string {\n    return MapPath(LocalMapName(name, id))\n}\n```\nFor example, the per-endpoint policy map is named `cilium_policy_v2_<endpoint_id>` (MapName = `\"cilium_policy_v2_\"` in `policymap.go:34`), accessed via `bpf.LocalMapPath(policymap.MapName, e.ID)` (`endpoint/bpf.go:88`).\n\nThe `lxcmap` global endpoint table (MapName = `\"cilium_lxc\"`, `lxcmap.go:21`) is also node-local \u2014 it maps endpoint IPs to their BPF metadata for that specific node's kernel.\n\n### Isolation Mechanism\n\nThe bpffs is a per-node host filesystem. The Cilium agent DaemonSet pod mounts the host's `/sys/fs/bpf` via `hostPath` volumes. Each node's bpffs is physically separate from every other node's.\n\nEach call to `reloadEndpoint()` pins maps under `/sys/fs/bpf/tc/globals/<mapname>` on the local node's filesystem. No other node can see or modify these pins.\n\nPer-endpoint bpffs directories are managed via `bpffsEndpointLinksDir()` and `bpffsEndpointDir()` (referenced in loader.go:546, 766, 770). When an endpoint is deleted, `Unload()` (`loader.go:735`) removes these directories with `bpf.Remove()`.\n\n### Effect of Map Failure on Other Nodes\n\nIf a node fails to create or update a BPF map (e.g., out of memory, kernel resource limits), the error surfaces only in that node's agent:\n\n- `policymap.OpenOrCreate(e.policyMapPath())` (`bpf.go:739`) returns an error if map creation fails; `runPreCompilationSteps()` propagates that error, aborting regeneration for that endpoint on that node only.\n- `lxcmap.WriteEndpoint()` (`bpf.go:473`) failures similarly return a local error.\n- Other nodes' agents continue using their own independently-created maps; they are completely unaffected.\n\nThe kernel BPF map subsystem is per-node. Cilium's shared cluster state (identity allocation, node IPs) is maintained in etcd/KVStore and propagated to each node's **user-space** agent, which then programs its own kernel maps locally.\n\n---\n\n## Evidence\n\n| File | Lines | Relevance |\n|------|-------|-----------|\n| `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go` | 134\u2013157, 170\u2013197 | `getBPFCPU()` probes local kernel # Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation

## Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle

### Component Responsible for Compilation

The **`loader`** struct in `pkg/datapath/loader/loader.go` (line 74) is the central component that orchestrates eBPF compilation, loading, and attachment on each node. It wraps the `objectCache` (defined in `pkg/datapath/loader/cache.go`) which amortizes compilation across endpoints with the same configuration.

The compilation itself is performed by **`clang`** (the LLVM C compiler), invoked via the `compile()` function in `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go` (line 170). The compiler is a node-local process – it runs as a subprocess of the Cilium agent on each node and produces BPF ELF object files.

### Node-Specific Configuration Affecting Compilation

Node-specific factors are embedded into BPF compilation in several ways:

1. **CPU/ISA capabilities**: `getBPFCPU()` (`compile.go:135`) probes the local kernel at startup (via `probes.HaveV3ISA()`, `probes.HaveV2ISA()`, `probes.HaveProgramHelper()`) to select the BPF instruction set (`v1`, `v2`, or `v3`). This is passed as `-mcpu=<version>` to clang.

2. **Number of CPUs**: `compile()` calls `ebpf.PossibleCPU()` and embeds the result as `-D__NR_CPUS__=<n>` (compile.go:191).

3. **Node configuration header**: `Reinitialize()` (`base.go:382`) writes a `node_config.h` header via `l.writeNodeConfigHeader(cfg)` (base.go:465), embedding the node's IP addresses, device indices, and feature flags. This header is included by all BPF programs at compile time.

4. **Template hashing**: `objectCache.fetchOrCompile()` (`cache.go:167`) hashes the combination of `LocalNodeConfiguration` and `EndpointConfiguration` to produce a cache key. Endpoints with different configurations get different compiled objects.

# Cilium eBPF Fault Isolation

## Q1: Per-Node eBPF Lifecycle

### Component Responsible for Compilation

The **`loader`** struct in `pkg/datapath/loader/loader.go` (line 74) is the central component that orchestrates eBPF compilation, loading, and attachment on each node. It wraps the `objectCache` (defined in `pkg/datapath/loader/cache.go`) which amortizes compilation across endpoints with the same configuration.

The compilation itself is performed by **`clang`** (the LLVM C compiler), invoked via the `compile()` function in `pkg/datapath/loader/compile.go` (line 170). The compiler is a node-local process — it runs as a subprocess of the Cilium agent on each node and produces BPF ELF object files.

### Node-Specific Configuration Affecting Compilation

Node-specific factors are embedded into BPF compilation in several ways:

1. **CPU/ISA capabilities**: `getBPFCPU()` (`compile.go:135`) probes the local kernel at startup (via `probes.HaveV3ISA()`, `probes.HaveV2ISA()`, `probes.HaveProgramHelper()`) to select the BPF instruction set (`v1`, `v2`, or `v3`). This is passed as `-mcpu=<version>` to clang.

2. **Number of CPUs**: `compile()` calls `ebpf.PossibleCPU()` and embeds the result as `-D__NR_CPUS__=<n>` (compile.go:191).

3. **Node configuration header**: `Reinitialize()` (`base.go:382`) writes a `node_config.h` header via `l.writeNodeConfigHeader(cfg)` (base.go:465), embedding the node's IP addresses, device indices, and feature flags. This header is included by all BPF programs at compile time.

4. **Template hashing**: `objectCache.fetchOrCompile()` (`cache.go:167`) hashes the combination of `LocalNodeConfiguration` and `EndpointConfiguration` to produce a cache key. Endpoints with different configurations get different compiled objects.
